reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
from transformers import AutoModelForCausalLM, AutoConfig

class Model(torch.nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, model_name, config):
        super().__init__()
        self.model_name = model_name
        self.config = config
        self.model = AutoModelForCausalLM.from_pretrained(self.model_name, config=self.config)

    def forward(self, x):
        return self.model(x).logits

model_name = "facebook/bart-large"
config = AutoConfig.from_pretrained(model_name)
vocab_size = config.vocab_size
sequence_length = 256
batch_size = 32

def get_inputs():
    inputs = torch.randint(0, vocab_size, (batch_size, sequence_length))
    return [inputs]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [model_name, config]

The key insight in transformer optimization is that much of the time is spent on: 1. QKV projection (3 matmuls or 1 big matmul) 2. Attention computation (QK^T, softmax, AV) 3. Output projection 4. FFN (2 matmuls) 5. Layer norms PyTorch/Transformers does these as separate operations, meaning there are many kernel launches and intermediate reads/writes to HBM. On an H100 with 3.35 TB/s bandwidth, a 406M parameter model with batch 32, seq 256 would have: - Activations per layer: ~32 * 256 * 1024 * 4 bytes * (several tensors) ≈ a few MB per layer - Total memory traffic for 12 layers: if each layer writes ~10MB of intermediates, that's ~120MB of writes per forward pass. Plus reading weights: 406M params * 4 bytes ≈ 1.6GB. So weight loading dominates. Wait, actually for batch=32, seq=256, hidden=1024: - Self-attention Q/K/V projections: W is (1024, 3072). Output shape: (32*256, 3072). Matmul: 32*256*1024*3072 = 25.7B ops. But PyTorch fuses this into one matmul. - Actually for 12 layers: 12 * 25.7B = 308B ops for QKV alone. - Output projection: 12 * 32*256*1024*1024 = 102B ops - FFN1: 12 * 32*256*1024*4096 = 407B ops - FFN2: 12 * 32*256*4096*1024 = 407B ops - Total GEMM: ~1.2T ops At 989 TFLOPS (TF32 tensor cores), this would take ~1.2ms. At 67 TFLOPS (FP32), ~18ms. The attention mechanism (non-GEMM parts) would add some time. PyTorch transformers typically runs BART large at maybe 10-50ms for this batch size. So there's room for optimization, but writing the whole thing in CUDA... Actually wait, if the model is already using the GPU in PyTorch with cuBLAS and optimized kernels, can we really beat it significantly? PyTorch pretty much uses the optimal cuBLAS GEMM kernels. Actually, 256 is small enough that the full attention scores for one head fit in shared memory: 256 * 256 * 4 bytes = 256KB, which exceeds the 227KB shared memory limit. So that won't fit. But I can tile it. Each block handles one (batch, head) and computes output rows in chunks. For example, process 32 query rows at a time: - Load 32 x 64 Q into smem (~8KB) - Iterate over K/V tiles of size say 64: - Load 64 x 64 K tile, compute 32 x 64 scores - Online softmax update - Load 64 x 64 V tile, accumulate weighted sum - This is very similar to FlashAttention but simplified. For 256 seq length and 64 head dim, with tile size 64: - Q tile: 32 x 64 = 2,048 floats = 8KB - K tile: 64 x 64 = 4,096 floats = 16KB - V tile: 64 x 64 = 4,096 floats = 16KB - Accumulator: 32 x 64 = 2,048 floats = 8KB - Max/sum: 32 x 2 = 64 floats = 256 bytes Total per block: ~48KB of smem. Very manageable. Even better, I can use warp-level matrix multiply with Tensor Cores (WMMA or wmma). But that's more complex. For a first version, I can use simple shared memory tiling. The kernel: ```cuda __global__ void causal_attention_kernel( const float* q, const float* k, const float* v, float* output, int batch_size, int num_heads, int seq_len, int head_dim ) { // Each block handles one (batch, head) pair // Each thread handles one query position // Load Q for this query position // Iterate over all key positions (causal: only 0..query_pos) // Compute dot product with K // Online softmax // Compute weighted sum with V // Write output // For seq_len=256 and head_dim=64, we can have 256 threads per block // Q for one query is 64 floats (256B) // We process all query positions in parallel across blocks // But actually each (batch, head) has 256 queries // If we use 256 threads per block and process one (batch, head), each thread handles one query } ``` Hmm, with 32 batch * 16 heads = 512 blocks, and 256 threads per block, each thread computes the attention for one query position. Let me design it properly: For one (batch, head): - 256 query positions - 64 head dim - We use 256 threads per block - Each thread handles exactly one query position Algorithm per block: 1. Load Q_tile for all queries: 256 x 64 = 16,384 floats = 64KB. Store in shared memory. Each thread loads its own Q row. 2. Process keys in chunks (say chunk_size = 64): a. Load K and V tiles for key positions 0..63, 64..127, etc. into shared memory. b. But wait, for causal mask, query i only attends to keys 0..i. So for threads with query_idx < chunk_start, they skip the entire chunk. c. Normalize output by sum_exp 4. Write output For seq_len=256 and head_dim=64, this could work. But the online softmax needs to handle the entire range. Actually, since each thread processes all keys up to its query index independently, we can do a simple softmax: - compute all attention scores - find max - compute exp(score - max) - sum exps - normalize For query_idx=255, it needs 256 dot products and 256 exps. That's fine for a single thread. Wait, but 256 dot products each of length 64 means 16,384 multiply-adds. At ~1 TFLOPS per CUDA core, that's ~16 microseconds. For 512 blocks, each with 256 threads, total latency might be a few hundred microseconds. Not bad. But the memory bandwidth of loading K and V: for each chunk of 64 keys, we load K and V: 2 * 64 * 64 = 8,192 floats = 32KB. 512 blocks * 4 chunks = 2,048 loads of 32KB each? No, blocks run in parallel. With ~114 SMs on H100, and maybe 2 blocks per SM (limited by smem: 227KB / 96KB ≈ 2 blocks), that's ~228 blocks concurrently. Total memory bandwidth needed: 228 * 32KB per chunk read, but that's from L2/shared after the first load. Actually, each SM handles multiple chunks sequentially. Global memory reads: each block reads 64KB (Q) + 4 * 32KB (K,V) = 192KB. 512 blocks total = 96MB. At 3.35TB/s that's 28 microseconds. Plus compute. Very doable. This is actually a quite reasonable kernel design! For the online softmax, I'll do: ```cpp float max_val = -INFINITY; float sum_exp = 0.0f; float out_regs[64] = {0}; for (int k = 0; k <= query_idx; ++k) { float dot = 0; for (int d = 0; d < 64; ++d) { dot += q_local[d] * k_shared[k % chunk_size][d]; } dot *= scale; // 1/sqrt(64) float old_max = max_val; max_val = fmaxf(max_val, dot); float exp_val = expf(dot - max_val); sum_exp = sum_exp * expf(old_max - max_val) + exp_val; // accumulate V weighted by exp_val (but need to normalize at the end) // Actually, we need to accumulate with the weight, then divide by sum_exp at the end for (int d = 0; d < 64; ++d) { out_regs[d] = out_regs[d] * expf(old_max - max_val) + exp_val * v_shared[k % chunk_size][d]; } } // Normalize for (int d = 0; d < 64; ++d) { out_regs[d] /= sum_exp; } ``` Wait, the rescaling of out_regs needs to be done carefully. If we have: At step i, old max = m_{i-1}, new max = m_i = max(m_{i-1}, x_i). We want to update the running sum: S_i = S_{i-1} * exp(m_{i-1} - m_i) + exp(x_i - m_i). And the output accumulator: O_i = O_{i-1} * (S_{i-1} * exp(m_{i-1} - m_i)) / S_i + ... no wait. Actually, the standard online softmax approach for attention output is: We maintain: - m (max so far) - l (sum of exp(x - m_current)) but this gets tricky A simpler approach: do two passes or use the FlashAttention-style online softmax. For simplicity and correctness, since seq_len is only 256, I can just accumulate the attention scores in a local array of 256 floats (1KB) in registers or shared memory, then do softmax, then compute the weighted sum of V. With 255 max registers per thread, and head_dim=64 already using 64 registers, plus 256 for scores = 320 floats. That's too much for registers. But I can store the 256 scores in shared memory per thread? No, shared memory is shared across the block. Actually, I can do a two-pass approach per thread: Pass 1: compute all scores, track max Pass 2: compute softmax weights Pass 3: accumulate weighted V But storing 256 scores per thread would require 256 threads * 256 floats = 262KB per block, which exceeds shared memory. So I should use the online approach more carefully. The FlashAttention online softmax updates the output as follows: ``` m_i = max(m_{i-1}, x_i) l_i = l_{i-1} * exp(m_{i-1} - m_i) + exp(x_i - m_i) o_i = o_{i-1} * (l_{i-1} * exp(m_{i-1} - m_i)) / l_i + (exp(x_i - m_i) / l_i) * v_i ``` Hmm, actually if I keep `exp_sum` as the sum of `exp(x - current_max)`, then when max changes, I need to rescale both the sum and the output. Let me use the formulation from FlashAttention: Initialize `m = -inf`, `l = 0`, `o = 0` (vector of head_dim). For each key j: `x_j = q · k_j` `m_new = max(m, x_j)` `l = l * exp(m - m_new) + exp(x_j - m_new)` `o = o * (l_old * exp(m - m_new)) / l_new + exp(x_j - m_new) / l_new * v_j` `m = m_new` Wait, if `l_old` is the old sum (based on old max), and `l_new` should be `l_old * exp(m_old - m_new) + exp(x_j - m_new)`. Then: `o_new = (o_old * l_old * exp(m_old - m_new) + exp(x_j - m_new) * v_j) / l_new` This makes sense because `o_old` is actually `sum_{prev} exp(x_prev - m_old) * v_prev / l_old`, so `o_old * l_old = sum_{prev} exp(x_prev - m_old) * v_prev`. Then after rescaling by `exp(m_old - m_new)`, we get `sum_{prev} exp(x_prev - m_new) * v_prev`. Adding `exp(x_j - m_new) * v_j` gives the new unnormalized sum. Dividing by `l_new` gives the new normalized output. So: ```cpp float l = 0; float m = -INFINITY; float o[64] = {0}; for (int j = 0; j <= query_idx; ++j) { float x = 0; for (int d = 0; d < 64; ++d) x += q[d] * k[j][d]; x *= scale; float m_new = max(m, x); float exp_m_diff = expf(m - m_new); float exp_x = expf(x - m_new); float l_new = l * exp_m_diff + exp_x; for (int d = 0; d < 64; ++d) { o[d] = (o[d] * l * exp_m_diff + exp_x * v[j][d]) / l_new; } m = m_new; l = l_new; } ``` This is exact (up to floating point order) and requires no extra storage. And it does compute in the right order. Note that dividing by `l_new` each iteration is fine. At the end, `o` contains `sum_j exp(x_j - m_final) * v_j / l_final`, which is correct because `softmax(x_j) = exp(x_j - m_final) / l_final`. Wait, actually no. The division by `l_new` inside the loop means `o` after step j represents the correctly normalized output for keys 0..j. At step j+1, we multiply by `l * exp_m_diff / l_new` (where l is the old l based on m). But `o[d] * l` is the unnormalized sum. So: `o_new_unnormalized = o_old[d] * l_old * exp_m_diff + exp_x * v[j][d]` `o_new = o_new_unnormalized / l_new` Yes, this is correct! Since `o_old[d] * l_old = sum_{prev} exp(x_prev - m_old) * v_prev[d]`, multiplying by `exp(m_old - m_new)` gives `sum_{prev} exp(x_prev - m_new) * v_prev[d]`. Adding `exp(x_j - m_new) * v[j][d]` and dividing by `l_new` gives the correct normalized average. At the end, `o` is correct. No final division needed. This is a beautiful online softmax algorithm! And it only requires keeping track of `m`, `l`, and `o[64]`. Total per thread: 66 floats + some temporaries. Very compact. Great, so the attention kernel can be implemented correctly and efficiently. For loading K and V, since we iterate over j from 0 to query_idx, and each thread needs all K/V for j up to query_idx, the most efficient approach is to have the block load K and V tiles into shared memory cooperatively. But here's the thing: different threads in the same block have different `query_idx`. Thread with query_idx=0 only needs j=0, thread with query_idx=255 needs j=0..255. We process K/V in tiles (say 64 elements). All threads in the block can use the same K/V tile loaded into shared memory. So the block-level algorithm: - Block processes one (batch, head) - 256 threads, thread i handles query i - Each thread loads Q[i][0:64] into its own register array (or shared memory slot) - For tile_start = 0; tile_start < seq_len; tile_start += tile_size: - Load K[tile_start : tile_start+tile_size][0:64] into shared memory cooperatively - Load V[tile_start : tile_start+tile_size][0:64] into shared memory cooperatively - __syncthreads() - Each thread: for j = tile_start; j < min(tile_start + tile_size, query_idx+1); j++: - compute x = dot(Q[i], K[j]) - update online softmax and accumulate V[j] - __syncthreads() This is very efficient! The K/V tiles are loaded once and reused by all 256 queries. For tile_size=64: - K_shared: 64 x 64 floats = 16KB - V_shared: 64 x 64 floats = 16KB Total shared memory = 32KB. Very good. And 256 threads per block. Occupancy on H100: Each SM can have up to 2048 threads. With 256 threads/block, that's 8 blocks per SM. But limited by shared memory: if each block uses 64KB (Q shared + K shared + V shared), then 227KB/64KB ≈ 3 blocks per SM. With 114 SMs, that's 342 blocks concurrently. We have 512 blocks total, so ~1.5 waves. Excellent. Wait, do I need Q in shared memory? Each thread only needs its own Q row, and it can keep it in registers. So no Q shared memory needed! That saves 64KB. So shared memory = K_shared (16KB) + V_shared (16KB) = 32KB. Blocks per SM = 227KB / 32KB ≈ 7 (but max is 32). So ~7 blocks per SM. Total concurrent blocks = 114 * 7 = 798 blocks. We only have 512 blocks, so all blocks run concurrently! That's amazing. Wait, but 7 * 256 = 1792 threads per SM, which is under 2048. So occupancy is good. This kernel design should be very efficient for our problem size! In `BartDecoderLayer.__init__`: ```python self.self_attn = BartAttention(...) self.dropout = config.dropout self.activation_fn = ACT2FN[config.activation_function] self.activation_dropout = config.activation_dropout self.self_attn_layer_norm = nn.LayerNorm(self.embed_dim) self.encoder_attn = BartAttention(...) # cross attention self.encoder_attn_layer_norm = nn.LayerNorm(self.embed_dim) self.fc1 = nn.Linear(self.embed_dim, config.decoder_ffn_dim) self.fc2 = nn.Linear(config.decoder_ffn_dim, self.embed_dim) self.final_layer_norm = nn.LayerNorm(self.embed_dim) ``` And in `forward`: ```python residual = hidden_states hidden_states, self_attn_weights, present_key_value = self.self_attn( hidden_states=hidden_states, attention_mask=attention_mask, ... ) hidden_states = nn.functional.dropout(hidden_states, p=self.dropout, training=self.training) hidden_states = residual + hidden_states hidden_states = self.self_attn_layer_norm(hidden_states) # Cross attention (if encoder_hidden_states is not None) ... residual = hidden_states hidden_states = self.activation_fn(self.fc1(hidden_states)) hidden_states = nn.functional.dropout(hidden_states, p=self.activation_dropout, training=self.training) hidden_states = self.fc2(hidden_states) hidden_states = nn.functional.dropout(hidden_states, p=self.dropout, training=self.training) hidden_states = residual + hidden_states hidden_states = self.final_layer_norm(hidden_states) ``` Yes, so it's: 1. No cross-attention in our case. Actually, to be safe, I can just use `torch.nn.functional.gelu` in my Python implementation, or I can write the exact GELU in CUDA. The exact GELU is `0.5 * x * (1 + erf(x / sqrt(2)))`. The approximate is `0.5 * x * (1 + tanh(sqrt(2/pi) * (x + 0.044715 * x^3)))`. I think PyTorch's `torch.nn.functional.gelu(approximate='none')` uses the exact formulation. For example: ```cpp torch::Tensor bart_forward( torch::Tensor input_ids, torch::Tensor embed_tokens_weight, torch::Tensor embed_positions_weight, // ... all other weights ) { // Embedding auto inputs_embeds = at::embedding(embed_tokens_weight, input_ids); auto positions = at::embedding(embed_positions_weight, input_ids); auto hidden = inputs_embeds * 32.0f + positions; // sqrt(1024) = 32 hidden = at::layer_norm(hidden, {hidden.size(-1)}, layernorm_embedding_weight, layernorm_embedding_bias, 1e-5); for (int i = 0; i < 12; i++) { // QKV auto q = at::addmm(q_bias[i], hidden, q_weight[i].t()); auto k = at::addmm(k_bias[i], hidden, k_weight[i].t()); auto v = at::addmm(v_bias[i], hidden, v_weight[i].t()); // Attention q = q.view({32, 256, 16, 64}).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}); k = k.view({32, 256, 16, 64}).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}); v = v.view({32, 256, 16, 64}).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}); auto attn_out = custom_attention_cuda(q, k, v); attn_out = attn_out.permute({0, 2, 1, 3}).contiguous().view({32, 256, 1024}); // Output proj auto out = at::addmm(out_bias[i], attn_out, out_weight[i].t()); hidden = at::add(hidden, out); // residual hidden = at::layer_norm(hidden, {hidden.size(-1)}, sa_ln_weight[i], sa_ln_bias[i], 1e-5); // FFN auto ffn1 = at::addmm(fc1_bias[i], hidden, fc1_weight[i].t()); ffn1 = at::gelu(ffn1); auto ffn2 = at::addmm(fc2_bias[i], ffn1, fc2_weight[i].t()); hidden = at::add(hidden, ffn2); // residual hidden = at::layer_norm(hidden, {hidden.size(-1)}, final_ln_weight[i], final_ln_bias[i], 1e-5); } hidden = at::layer_norm(hidden, {hidden.size(-1)}, final_layer_norm_weight, final_layer_norm_bias, 1e-5); auto logits = at::addmm(lm_head_bias, hidden, lm_head_weight.t()); return logits; } ``` But there are problems: 1. `at::layer_norm` exists but requires specific parameters. 2. `at::gelu` exists. 3. The `custom_attention_cuda` needs to be a custom kernel. 4. Intermediate allocations (`q`, `k`, `v`, etc.) happen at each layer. For 12 layers, that's a lot of allocations. 5. The `permute` and `contiguous` and `view` operations also allocate or at least create new tensor metadata. In Python, the same allocations happen. The difference with C++ is just less Python overhead. But for speed, the allocations are costly. In `torch.no_grad()` mode, PyTorch uses caching allocator, so allocations of the same size are fast. But for 12 layers, with 6-8 intermediate tensors per layer, that's ~72-96 allocations per forward. At a few microseconds each, that's maybe 0.5ms total. Not huge. The actual computation dominates. Let me plan out the code structure: ```python import torch from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = r''' __global__ void causal_attention_kernel(...) { // ... implementation } __global__ void fused_residual_layernorm_kernel(...) { // ... implementation } torch::Tensor causal_attention_forward(torch::Tensor q, torch::Tensor k, torch::Tensor v) { // check tensors auto output = torch::empty_like(q); // launch kernel return output; } torch::Tensor fused_residual_layernorm_forward(torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor residual, torch::Tensor weight, torch::Tensor bias) { auto output = torch::empty_like(x); // launch kernel return output; } ''' cpp_sources = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> torch::Tensor causal_attention_forward(torch::Tensor q, torch::Tensor k, torch::Tensor v) { // ... } torch::Tensor fused_residual_layernorm_forward(torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor residual, torch::Tensor weight, torch::Tensor bias) { // ... } ''' module = load_inline( name='bart_cuda', cpp_sources=cpp_sources, cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=['causal_attention_forward', 'fused_residual_layernorm_forward'], extra_cuda_cflags=['--use_fast_math', '-O3'], extra_cflags=['-O3'], ) class ModelNew(torch.nn.Module): def __init__(self, model_name, config): super().__init__() from transformers import AutoModelForCausalLM self.model = AutoModelForCausalLM.from_pretrained(model_name, config=config) self.model.eval() self.model.cuda() # Extract weights decoder = self.model.model.decoder self.embed_tokens = decoder.embed_tokens.weight.data.cuda() self.embed_positions = decoder.embed_positions.weight.data.cuda() # ... etc def forward(self, x): x = x.cuda() # ... call kernels and PyTorch ops return logits ``` Wait, `load_inline` has specific requirements. The `cpp_sources` should contain the function definitions, and `cuda_sources` should contain the CUDA code. But actually, for `load_inline`, the CUDA kernels must be declared in the C++ source or the CUDA source. Let me be careful about the structure. Actually, looking at the PyTorch docs for `load_inline`: - `cuda_sources`: CUDA/C++ source code containing kernel definitions - `cpp_sources`: C++ source code containing wrapper functions that launch kernels The wrapper functions should be in `cpp_sources` and the CUDA kernels in `cuda_sources`. But `cpp_sources` can also contain kernel launch code if it includes the kernel declarations. Alternatively, I can put everything in `cpp_sources` and use `__global__` there? So the typical pattern: ```python cuda_source = ''' __global__ void my_kernel(...) { ... } ''' cpp_source = ''' #include <torch/extension.h> torch::Tensor my_forward(torch::Tensor x) { my_kernel<<<...>>>(...); return x; } ''' ``` But actually, since `nvcc` can compile code with `__global__` inside, and the linking is done automatically, I can define kernels in `cuda_sources` and call them from `cpp_sources`. To make the wrappers see the kernels, I need forward declarations in `cpp_sources`. ```python cpp_source = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> // Forward declarations of CUDA kernels void launch_causal_attention(const float* q, const float* k, const float* v, float* out, ...); void launch_fused_residual_layernorm(...); torch::Tensor causal_attention_forward(torch::Tensor q, torch::Tensor k, torch::Tensor v) { auto out = torch::empty_like(q); launch_causal_attention(q.data_ptr<float>(), ..., out.data_ptr<float>(), ...); return out; } ''' cuda_source = r''' __global__ void causal_attention_kernel(...) { ... } void launch_causal_attention(const float* q, const float* k, const float* v, float* out, ...) { causal_attention_kernel<<<grid, block>>>(...); } __global__ void fused_residual_layernorm_kernel(...) { ... } void launch_fused_residual_layernorm(...) { fused_residual_layernorm_kernel<<<grid, block>>>(...); } ''' ``` This should work! The C++ file declares the launcher functions, and the CUDA file defines the kernels and the launcher functions. The linker connects them. OK, let me now write the actual kernel code. **Causal Attention Kernel:** For Q, K, V shaped as [B, H, S, D] = [32, 16, 256, 64] Grid: (B * H) blocks Block: S threads (256 threads) Shared memory per block: - K_tile: [tile_size, D] floats - V_tile: [tile_size, D] floats Since S=256, I can set tile_size = 64. That's 64*64 = 4,096 floats = 16KB per buffer. Total 32KB per block. ```cuda __global__ void causal_attention_kernel(const float* q, const float* k, const float* v, float* out, int B, int H, int S, int D) { int bh = blockIdx.x; // which (batch, head) int s = threadIdx.x; // which query position if (s >= S) return; float scale = 1.0f / sqrtf((float)D); // Load Q for this thread's query position float q_vals[64]; // We'll unroll if possible, otherwise loop const float* q_ptr = q + bh * S * D + s * D; for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { q_vals[d] = q_ptr[d]; } float m = -INFINITY; float l = 0.0f; float o[64]; for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) o[d] = 0.0f; __shared__ float k_tile[64][64]; // [tile_size, D] __shared__ float v_tile[64][64]; // [tile_size, D] int num_tiles = (S + 63) / 64; // 4 tiles for (int tile = 0; tile < num_tiles; tile++) { int tile_start = tile * 64; // Load K and V tiles cooperatively for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < 64 * D; idx += blockDim.x) { int row = idx / D; int col = idx % D; int global_idx = bh * S * D + (tile_start + row) * D + col; if (tile_start + row < S) { k_tile[row][col] = k[global_idx]; v_tile[row][col] = v[global_idx]; } else { k_tile[row][col] = 0.0f; v_tile[row][col] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); // Process keys in this tile int key_end = min(tile_start + 64, S); for (int key_pos = tile_start; key_pos < key_end; key_pos++) { if (key_pos > s) break; // causal mask: only attend to positions <= s // Compute dot product float dot = 0.0f; for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { dot += q_vals[d] * k_tile[key_pos - tile_start][d]; } dot *= scale; // Online softmax update float m_new = fmaxf(m, dot); float exp_m_diff = expf(m - m_new); float exp_dot = expf(dot - m_new); float l_new = l * exp_m_diff + exp_dot; float v_val; for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { v_val = v_tile[key_pos - tile_start][d]; o[d] = (o[d] * l * exp_m_diff + exp_dot * v_val) / l_new; } m = m_new; l = l_new; } __syncthreads(); } // Write output float* out_ptr = out + bh * S * D + s * D; for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { out_ptr[d] = o[d]; } } ``` Wait, I need to be careful about the shared memory arrays with variable dimensions. CUDA doesn't like `__shared__ float k_tile[64][64]` inside the kernel when 64 is a variable? Actually, it should be fine since it's a compile-time constant. Let me use `__shared__ float k_tile[4096];` and index manually to be safe. Also, I need to make sure `blockDim.x` is at least 64 to load the tile. With 256 threads, that's fine. One optimization: since `D=64` is fixed for this model (1024 hidden / 16 heads), I could hardcode it and unroll the loop. But to make the kernel slightly more general, I'll keep D as a parameter. But for performance, unrolling the inner loop over d=64 would help a lot. Let me use `#pragma unroll` or manual unrolling if I hardcode. Actually, for now I'll keep it as a loop. Actually wait, 64 iterations is small. The compiler might unroll it automatically. One issue: `for (int d = 0; d < D; d++)` with D passed as a runtime parameter won't be unrolled. I can pass D as a template parameter if I want, but that's more complex. For seq=256 and D=64, the performance is probably fine anyway since we're memory-bound. Another consideration: I should process the queries where `s < S` but with `blockDim.x = 256` and `S = 256`, every thread has valid work. For keys beyond `s`, we break out of the loop. This means threads with smaller `s` do less work. This creates load imbalance within the block. For example, thread 0 only processes key 0, while thread 255 processes keys 0-255. To fix this, I could assign different query positions to different blocks instead of threads. For example, each block handles one (batch, head), and within the block, threads process multiple query positions or cooperate on one query. Actually, thread-level load imbalance is bad but maybe manageable for S=256 since the total work is O(S^2) and the average thread does O(S^2 / S) = O(S) work. Thread 0 does 1 key, thread 255 does 256 keys. The block is only done when thread 255 finishes. So the block's runtime is determined by the maximum thread work, which is 256 dot products and 256 softmax updates. That's 256 * 64 = 16K multiply-adds. On a GPU, even one thread can do this fairly quickly, but 256 threads with increasing loads means the block is idle waiting for one thread. A better approach: use more blocks. Each block handles one query position for all heads/batches. So: - Grid: (B, H, S) blocks - Block: some number of threads that cooperate on one dot product But then for each (batch, head, query) block, we need to load all K and V for keys 0..query_pos. If each block loads a tile of keys independently, we lose the benefit of sharing K/V across queries. And we'd have B*H*S = 32*16*256 = 131,072 blocks! That might be too many blocks with too little work each. Actually, H100 can handle many blocks quickly. But the overhead of launching 131K blocks might be noticeable. Alternatively, I can keep one block per (batch, head) = 512 blocks, and within each block, have threads cooperate on multiple queries. For example, each block handles a tile of queries (say 64 queries) and all keys. But then I need to handle the softmax accumulation per query, which can't be easily parallelized across keys if I want the exact online algorithm. Hmm. Actually, for small D (64) and small S (256), the load imbalance might not be that bad. A single thread doing 256 dot products of 64 elements each is 16,384 floating point ops. At ~1 TFLOPS per SM across 32 threads per warp? No, a single thread is much slower. But with 32 threads in a warp, if they all do different amounts of work, the warp is limited by the slowest thread. For thread 255 doing 256 iterations, and threads doing fewer, the utilization is roughly 50% (triangle number / (S * S) = 128/256 = 0.5). But since only 1 warp has this issue if we use 256 threads? Actually, each block is 256 threads in one warp? No, 256 threads = 8 warps. Each warp has 32 threads with consecutive query positions. Wait, with 256 threads = 8 warps: - Warp 0: threads 0-31, query positions 0-31, max work 32 dot products - Warp 1: threads 32-63, query positions 32-63, max work 64 dot products - ... - Warp 7: threads 224-255, query positions 224-255, max work 256 dot products The block runtime is determined by warp 7, where thread 255 does 256 dot products. The other warps finish much faster and wait. Total runtime is determined by the slowest warp. Warp 7 time: 256 * (64 mult-add) = 16K iterations. Let's say each takes 1 cycle (ignoring memory), that's 16K cycles. At 2GHz, that's 8 microseconds. Memory might make it 20-30 microseconds. This is still quite fast. And since 512 blocks run concurrently on 114 SMs, each SM gets about 4-5 blocks. If each takes ~20 microseconds, total time for attention is a few hundred microseconds. That's totally fine. Actually, the amount of computation per block is: sum_{s=224}^{255} (s+1) dot products = roughly 256*32 = 8192 dot products. Each dot product is 64 mult-adds. Total per block: 8192 * 64 ≈ 524K FLOPs. For 512 blocks: ~268M FLOPs. At 67 TFLOPS FP32, that's ~4 microseconds. But memory bandwidth will dominate. Loading Q for all threads: 256 * 64 * 4 bytes = 64KB (already in registers). So the attention kernel should be very fast, maybe under 50 microseconds! OK, so the load imbalance is not a major issue for S=256. I'll proceed with this design. **Fused Residual + LayerNorm Kernel:** ```cuda __global__ void fused_residual_layernorm_kernel(const float* x, const float* residual, const float* weight, const float* bias, float* out, int N, int C, float eps) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; if (idx >= N) return; // x and residual shape: [N, C] // N = batch * seq = 8192 // C = 1024 const float* x_row = x + idx * C; const float* r_row = residual + idx * C; float* o_row = out + idx * C; // Compute residual + x float sum = 0.0f; float sq_sum = 0.0f; float vals[1024]; // Can't put 1024 floats in registers per thread! } ``` Wait, storing 1024 floats per thread is 4KB, which exceeds register space. I need a different approach. For layer norm over 1024 elements, I can have one block handle one row, with 1024 threads computing cooperatively. But N=8192 rows, so I'd need 8192 blocks. With 1024 threads per block, that's a lot. Actually, 8192 blocks of 1024 threads = 8,388,608 threads. On H100 with 114 SMs * 2048 threads/SM = 233,472 concurrent threads. So we'd need ~36 waves. That's fine. But 1024 threads per block might have too much overhead. Alternatively, I can use 256 threads per block and have each thread process 4 elements. Or I can use warp-level primitives for reduction. Actually, for 1024 elements, using 256 threads (1 block per row) where each thread handles 4 elements is efficient. ```cuda __global__ void fused_residual_layernorm_kernel(const float* x, const float* residual, const float* weight, const float* bias, float* out, int N, int C, float eps) { int row = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int num_threads = blockDim.x; if (row >= N) return; const float* x_ptr = x + row * C; const float* r_ptr = residual + row * C; float* o_ptr = out + row * C; __shared__ float sh_mean,sh_var; float local_sum = 0.0f; for (int i = tid; i < C; i += num_threads) { float val = x_ptr[i] + r_ptr[i]; local_sum += val; } // Warp reduction for sum #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { local_sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, local_sum, offset); } if (tid % 32 == 0) { // atomic add to shared mean or use shared mem reduction across warps } // Actually, use shared mem for block reduction } ``` Wait, a cleaner approach is to use `atomicAdd` to a shared memory float for the block reduction, but atomics can be slow for many warps. Better to do a two-level reduction. Or I can use the `Welford` algorithm but that requires two reductions (mean and M2). Actually, for simplicity, I'll use shared memory reductions: ```cuda __shared__ float s_sum[256]; __shared__ float s_sq_sum[256]; s_sum[tid] = local_sum; s_sq_sum[tid] = local_sq_sum; __syncthreads(); // Block reduction for (int s = blockDim.x / 2; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (tid < s) { s_sum[tid] += s_sum[tid + s]; s_sq_sum[tid] += s_sq_sum[tid + s]; } __syncthreads(); } float mean = s_sum[0] / C; float var = s_sq_sum[0] / C - mean * mean; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); for (int i = tid; i < C; i += num_threads) { float val = x_ptr[i] + r_ptr[i]; o_ptr[i] = (val - mean) * inv_std * weight[i] + bias[i]; } ``` Wait, `weight` and `bias` are also per-element. I need to make sure I access them by `i` not by row. Actually, layer norm's weight and bias are shape [C], so `weight[i]` is correct. For shared memory, I can use dynamically sized shared memory, or fixed size. Since I use 256 threads, `s_sum[256]` is fine. But let me think: do I need `s_sq_sum` to use the same array? No, I need two arrays. Or I can compute variance using `sum(x^2) / C - mean^2`. Local computation: ```cpp float local_sum = 0; float local_sq_sum = 0; for (int i = tid; i < C; i += num_threads) { float val = x_ptr[i] + r_ptr[i]; local_sum += val; local_sq_sum += val * val; } ``` Yes. One issue: `weight[i]` and `bias[i]` accesses. These are the same for all rows. Actually, thread 0 accesses `weight[0]`, thread 1 accesses `weight[1]`, etc. This IS coalesced! Great. So the layer norm kernel should work well. A forward pass could be ~10-15ms. - Our optimized version might be ~8-12ms. - Not a huge win, but maybe enough. Wait, I just realized that my forward will use `torch.addmm` inside a Python for loop of 12 iterations. This means 12 Python iterations, each calling several PyTorch ops. The Python overhead per op is small, but we have ~10 ops per layer * 12 layers = 120 PyTorch op calls. At ~5 microseconds per call, that's 0.6ms. Not huge. The actual computation is the bottleneck. OK, I think I need to take a slightly different strategy. What if I not only write custom kernels but also use a more memory-efficient layout? For example, instead of doing `hidden.view(B*S, hidden_size)` for matmuls, I could keep the tensor in [B, S, hidden] layout and use `einsum` or `bmm`. But `nn.Linear` expects [*, hidden] and does a 2D matmul internally, so it's equivalent. Another big win: `torch.addmm` with pre-allocated output buffer avoids one allocation per call. For 12 layers with ~5 matmuls each, that's 60 allocations saved. The PyTorch caching allocator handles this well, but direct buffer reuse is slightly better. I can pre-allocate buffers for all the intermediate activations. Since the shapes are fixed, this is easy. Let me write the final code structure. For pre-allocated buffers, I need: - q, k, v: [B*S, hidden] - actually, since we reshape them to [B, H, S, D] for attention, I could pre-allocate them as [B, H, S, D] - attn_out: [B, H, S, D] - ffn1: [B*S, ffn_dim] - ffn2: [B*S, hidden] - logits: [B*S, vocab_size] But I also need buffers for the 12 layers. Can I reuse the same buffers across layers? Yes! The computation is sequential, so we can overwrite the same buffer each layer. So in `__init__`: ```python self.buf_q = torch.empty(B, H, S, D, device='cuda', dtype=torch.float32) self.buf_k = torch.empty(B, H, S, D, device='cuda', dtype=torch.float32) self.buf_v = torch.empty(B, H, S, D, device='cuda', dtype=torch.float32) self.buf_attn = torch.empty(B, H, S, D, device='cuda', dtype=torch.float32) self.buf_ffn1 = torch.empty(B, S, ffn_dim, device='cuda', dtype=torch.float32) self.buf_ffn2 = torch.empty(B, S, hidden, device='cuda', dtype=torch.float32) self.buf_logits = torch.empty(B, S, vocab_size, device='cuda', dtype=torch.float32) ``` In `forward`: ```python # Projections torch.addmm(q_bias, hidden.view(-1, hidden), q_weight.t(), out=self.buf_q.view(-1, hidden)) self.buf_q = self.buf_q.view(B, S, H, D).permute(0, 2, 1, 3) # ... same for k, v # Attention custom_attention(self.buf_q, self.buf_k, self.buf_v, out=self.buf_attn) # Reshape back attn_proj_input = self.buf_attn.permute(0, 2, 1, 3).contiguous().view(-1, hidden) torch.addmm(out_bias, attn_proj_input, out_weight.t(), out=self.buf_out.view(-1, hidden)) self.buf_out = self.buf_out.view(B, S, hidden) # Fused residual + LN hidden = custom_residual_layernorm(self.buf_out, hidden, sa_ln_weight, sa_ln_bias) ``` Wait, `permute` returns a view, but `contiguous()` creates a copy. That's an allocation. To avoid this, I can pre-allocate `buf_q_transposed` and use `copy_` with permutation? No, `copy_` doesn't support permutation. Actually, for `permute`, it's just a metadata change. But my custom attention kernel expects the data in [B, H, S, D] layout. If `buf_q` is stored as [B, S, H, D] and I call `.permute(0, 2, 1, 3)`, the resulting tensor is non-contiguous with strides reflecting the permutation. But actually, I can just keep the buffer in [B, H, S, D] layout from the start. After `torch.addmm`, the output is [B*S, hidden] which is contiguous. If I `view` it to [B, S, H, D], that's a valid view if contiguous. Then I need to permute to [B, H, S, D]. To avoid the permutation, I can write the custom attention kernel to accept data in [B, S, H, D] layout where Q[b, s, h, d] = Q for batch b, sequence s, head h, dim d. Then I don't need to permute! Let me redesign the attention kernel for [B, S, H, D] layout. Actually, that's even simpler! In this layout, the innermost dimension is D, which is contiguous. For a given (b, h, s), the elements are at stride D for d. The next dimension is H with stride S*D. The next is S with stride H*D. Wait, if shape is [B, S, H, D]: - For batch b, seq s, head h: offset = b * S * H * D + s * H * D + h * D And the adjacent head is offset by D, adjacent seq is offset by H*D, adjacent batch is offset by S*H*D. For the attention kernel, if each block handles one (batch, head) pair and each thread handles one seq position: - Q for thread s: `q_ptr = q + b * S * H * D + s * H * D + h * D` - K for key k: `k_ptr = k + b * S * H * D + k * H * D + h * D` - V for key k: `v_ptr = v + b * S * H * D + k * H * D + h * D` This works great! No permutation needed. The strides are: - stride_s: H * D - stride_b: S * H * D So in the attention kernel, instead of computing `bh * S * D`, I compute `b * S * H * D + h * D`. And in Python, after `torch.addmm` to [B*S, hidden], I can view to [B, S, H, D] by doing: ```python q = torch.addmm(q_bias, hidden.view(-1, hidden), q_weight.t()) q = q.view(B, S, H, D) ``` This is a valid view because the output of `addmm` is contiguous and [B*S, H*D] views naturally to [B, S, H, D]. Then I pass `q` directly to the attention kernel. And the output of the attention kernel is in [B, S, H, D] layout, which I can view to [B*S, hidden] for the output projection. This eliminates all permute/contiguous operations. Great optimization! Similarly, for the layer norm kernel, the input is [B, S, hidden] = [32, 256, 1024]. The kernel operates on each row independently. OK let me refine the kernel code for [B, S, H, D] layout. ```cuda __global__ void causal_attention_kernel(const float* q, const float* k, const float* v, float* out, int B, int S, int H, int D) { int b = blockIdx.x / H; int h = blockIdx.x % H; int s = threadIdx.x; if (s >= S) return; int stride_s = H * D; int stride_b = S * H * D; const float* q_ptr = q + b * stride_b + s * stride_s + h * D; float q_vals[64]; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { q_vals[d] = q_ptr[d]; } float scale = 1.0f / sqrtf((float)D); float m = -INFINITY; float l = 0.0f; float o[64]; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) o[d] = 0.0f; __shared__ float k_tile[64][64]; __shared__ float v_tile[64][64]; int tile_size = 64; int num_tiles = (S + tile_size - 1) / tile_size; for (int tile = 0; tile < num_tiles; tile++) { int tile_start = tile * tile_size; // Cooperatively load K and V tiles for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < tile_size * D; idx += blockDim.x) { int row = idx / D; int col = idx % D; int global_k = b * stride_b + (tile_start + row) * stride_s + h * D + col; if (tile_start + row < S) { k_tile[row][col] = k[global_k]; v_tile[row][col] = v[global_k]; } else { k_tile[row][col] = 0.0f; v_tile[row][col] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); // Compute attention for keys in this tile int key_end = min(tile_start + tile_size, S); for (int key_pos = tile_start; key_pos < key_end; key_pos++) { if (key_pos > s) break; // causal float dot = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { dot += q_vals[d] * k_tile[key_pos - tile_start][d]; } dot *= scale; float m_new = fmaxf(m, dot); float exp_m_diff = expf(m - m_new); float exp_dot = expf(dot - m_new); float l_new = l * exp_m_diff + exp_dot; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { o[d] = (o[d] * l * exp_m_diff + exp_dot * v_tile[key_pos - tile_start][d]) / l_new; } m = m_new; l = l_new; } __syncthreads(); } float* out_ptr = out + b * stride_b + s * stride_s + h * D; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { out_ptr[d] = o[d]; } } ``` Wait, I used `float k_tile[64][64]` which is a compile-time constant, but D might be a variable. Since D is passed as a parameter, `k_tile[64][64]` with hardcoded 64 is fine as long as D is actually 64. If D is different, we'd have out-of-bounds. But for bart-large, D=64. I can make it more robust by declaring `extern __shared__ float smem[]` and manually indexing, or by using a template parameter. For simplicity and because the problem has fixed architecture, I'll just hardcode it to 64. But wait, if I hardcode 64, and D is passed as a runtime parameter, the compiler might optimize differently. Let me just pass D as a template parameter if possible? No, pybind11 doesn't easily handle templates. I'll just use a fixed maximum dimension with `#define MAX_HEAD_DIM 64` and assert that D <= MAX_HEAD_DIM. Or I can just hardcode the loop unrolls and array sizes. Actually, to keep it general and correct, I'll use the runtime D but keep the shared memory allocation dynamic. CUDA allows: ```cuda extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* k_tile = smem; float* v_tile = smem + tile_size * D; ``` And then I launch with `<<<grid, block, 2 * tile_size * D * sizeof(float)>>>`. This is much cleaner and works for any D. Let me rewrite with dynamic shared memory. But wait, for dynamic shared memory, I need to index as a 1D array: ```cuda int load_idx = threadIdx.x; while (load_idx < tile_size * D) { int row = load_idx / D; int col = load_idx % D; // ... k_tile[row * D + col] = ...; v_tile[row * D + col] = ...; load_idx += blockDim.x; } ``` And when accessing: `k_tile[(key_pos - tile_start) * D + d]`. This is cleaner. For q_vals and o, since D is known at compile time for bart-large (64), I can still use fixed-size arrays if I hardcode the maximum. But to be safe, I'll just use a loop with `#pragma unroll` if I use a small fixed size. Actually, for dynamic D, the compiler can't unroll the loop over q_vals. But for D=64, the loop is small enough. Let me use dynamic shared memory and register arrays sized by a fixed but sufficiently large constant: ```cuda #define MAX_D 64 __global__ void causal_attention_kernel(...) { float q_vals[MAX_D]; float o_vals[MAX_D]; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* k_tile = smem; float* v_tile = smem + tile_size * D; for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { q_vals[d] = q_ptr[d]; o_vals[d] = 0.0f; } ... for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { out_ptr[d] = o_vals[d]; } } ``` And launch with shared memory size: `2 * 64 * D * sizeof(float)` where D=64. Wait, tile_size is 64, so shared = 2 * 64 * 64 * sizeof(float) = 32KB. Actually, the tile_size should be a parameter too. But since S=256, using 64 is good. OK, I think my kernel design is solid. Q, K, V projections 2. Reshape 3. bmm for QK^T 4. Scale and mask 5. Softmax 6. Dropout 7. bmm for Attn @ V 8. Reshape 9. Output projection The dropout in step 6 is `self.dropout` from the attention module, which is the same `config.dropout` (typically 0.1). In eval mode, this dropout is disabled. Good. And the scaling is by `1/sqrt(head_dim)`. Good. Now, one subtlety: in `BartAttention`, the Q, K, V reshape is based on `tgt_len` and `bsz`. For self-attention, `key_states` and `value_states` come from the same input, so they have the same length. For `BartForCausalLM`, the attention uses a causal mask. In `BartDecoder`, the causal mask is created and passed to each layer. So only the causal mask is used. This mask has shape [bsz, 1, tgt_len, tgt_len] and contains 0 and large negative values (like -1e9 or -inf). The mask is then passed to each `BartDecoderLayer`, which passes it to `BartAttention`. In our custom implementation, we don't need to create this large mask. Our attention kernel inherently supports causal masking. Great. I'll structure the file like this: ```python import torch import math from transformers import AutoModelForCausalLM cuda_source = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <cmath> #define MAX_D 64 #define TILE_SIZE 64 __global__ void causal_attention_kernel(const float* q, const float* k, const float* v, float* out, int B, int S, int H, int D) { int bh = blockIdx.x; int b = bh / H; int h = bh % H; int s = threadIdx.x; if (s >= S) return; int stride_s = H * D; int stride_b = S * H * D; const float* q_ptr = q + b * stride_b + s * stride_s + h * D; float q_vals[MAX_D]; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { q_vals[d] = q_ptr[d]; } float scale = 1.0f / sqrtf((float)D); float m = -INFINITY; float l = 0.0f; float o[MAX_D]; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { o[d] = 0.0f; } extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* k_tile = smem; float* v_tile = smem + TILE_SIZE * D; int num_tiles = (S + TILE_SIZE - 1) / TILE_SIZE; for (int tile = 0; tile < num_tiles; tile++) { int tile_start = tile * TILE_SIZE; // Load K and V tiles cooperatively for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < TILE_SIZE * D; idx += blockDim.x) { int row = idx / D; int col = idx % D; int global_idx = b * stride_b + (tile_start + row) * stride_s + h * D + col; if (tile_start + row < S) { k_tile[row * D + col] = k[global_idx]; v_tile[row * D + col] = v[global_idx]; } else { k_tile[row * D + col] = 0.0f; v_tile[row * D + col] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); int key_end = min(tile_start + TILE_SIZE, S); for (int key_pos = tile_start; key_pos < key_end; key_pos++) { if (key_pos > s) break; float dot = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { dot += q_vals[d] * k_tile[(key_pos - tile_start) * D + d]; } dot *= scale; float m_new = fmaxf(m, dot); float exp_m_diff = expf(m - m_new); float exp_dot = expf(dot - m_new); float l_new = l * exp_m_diff + exp_dot; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { float v_val = v_tile[(key_pos - tile_start) * D + d]; o[d] = (o[d] * l * exp_m_diff + exp_dot * v_val) / l_new; } m = m_new; l = l_new; } __syncthreads(); } float* out_ptr = out + b * stride_b + s * stride_s + h * D; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { out_ptr[d] = o[d]; } } torch::Tensor causal_attention_forward(torch::Tensor q, torch::Tensor k, torch::Tensor v) { int B = q.size(0); int S = q.size(1); int H = q.size(2); int D = q.size(3); TORCH_CHECK(D <= MAX_D, "head_dim must be <= MAX_D"); auto out = torch::empty_like(q); int blocks = B * H; int threads = S; size_t smem_size = 2 * TILE_SIZE * D * sizeof(float); causal_attention_kernel<<<blocks, threads, smem_size>>>( q.data_ptr<float>(), k.data_ptr<float>(), v.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), B, S, H, D ); return out; } __global__ void fused_residual_layernorm_kernel(const float* x, const float* residual, const float* weight, const float* bias, float* out, int N, int C, float eps) { int row = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int num_threads = blockDim.x; if (row >= N) return; const float* x_ptr = x + row * C; const float* r_ptr = residual + row * C; float* o_ptr = out + row * C; __shared__ float s_sum[256]; __shared__ float s_sq_sum[256]; // Some thread blocks might have more or less than 256 threads. // We only use indices up to blockDim.x. float local_sum = 0.0f; float local_sq_sum = 0.0f; for (int i = tid; i < C; i += num_threads) { float val = x_ptr[i] + r_ptr[i]; local_sum += val; local_sq_sum += val * val; } s_sum[tid] = local_sum; s_sq_sum[tid] = local_sq_sum; __syncthreads(); // Tree reduction for (int stride = num_threads / 2; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) { if (tid < stride) { s_sum[tid] += s_sum[tid + stride]; s_sq_sum[tid] += s_sq_sum[tid + stride]; } __syncthreads(); } float mean = s_sum[0] / C; float var = s_sq_sum[0] / C - mean * mean; float inv_std = rsqrtf(var + eps); for (int i = tid; i < C; i += num_threads) { float val = x_ptr[i] + r_ptr[i]; o_ptr[i] = (val - mean) * inv_std * weight[i] + bias[i]; } } torch::Tensor fused_residual_layernorm_forward(torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor residual, torch::Tensor weight, torch::Tensor bias) { int N = x.size(0) * x.size(1); // batch * seq int C = x.size(2); // hidden_size auto out = torch::empty_like(x); int threads = 256; int blocks = N; fused_residual_layernorm_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>( x.data_ptr<float>(), residual.data_ptr<float>(), weight.data_ptr<float>(), bias.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), N, C, 1e-5f ); return out; } ''' cpp_sources = ''' #include <torch/extension.h> ''' # Actually, since I put everything in cuda_sources, cpp_sources can be empty or minimal. ``` Wait, I can't put `torch::Tensor` functions in `cuda_sources` if `cpp_sources` is compiled separately? Actually, I just realized: `load_inline` auto-generates a `PYBIND11_MODULE`. It looks for the functions listed in `functions=[...]` in the compiled code. It doesn't matter if they're in the CUDA or C++ source, as long as they have external linkage and are found by the linker. But some versions of PyTorch's `load_inline` might expect `cpp_sources` to contain the host functions. Let me check common patterns. Looking at PyTorch examples: ```python module = load_inline( name="inline_extension", cpp_sources=[cpp_source], cuda_sources=[cuda_source], functions=["forward"], extra_cuda_cflags=["--expt-relaxed-constexpr"], ) ``` In this pattern, `forward` is typically declared in `cpp_source`. The CUDA kernels are in `cuda_source`. But the names are linked together. So to follow the pattern properly, I should: 1. Put `__global__` kernels in `cuda_source` 2. Put host wrapper functions in `cpp_source` 3. Declare the kernels in `cpp_source` or the wrappers can call them if they're in the same translation unit... wait, if they're in different files, the C++ compiler needs to see the kernel declarations. To avoid this issue, I can put everything in `cpp_source` and use nvcc-specific features? No, the C++ compiler won't understand `__global__`. Or, I can put forward declarations in `cpp_source`: ```cpp // In cpp_source void causal_attention_kernel(...); // nvcc name mangling... ``` Actually, CUDA kernels have C++ linkage and their mangled names are complex. Using `extern "C"` for kernels isn't allowed for `__global__` functions in CUDA. Positions (0,1) and (1,1) are True. - Wait, but causal masking usually allows attending to past and present positions, i.e., j <= i. - Here `mask_cond` is the row index (i), and `mask_cond + 1` is the column. - `i < j + 1` means `i <= j`. Actually, in `transformers` library, attention is typically `attn_weights = Q @ K^T`. The shape is [bsz, heads, tgt_len, src_len], and position (i, j) represents query i attending to key j. A causal mask should prevent query i from attending to key j > i (future positions). So allowed positions are j <= i. In the mask tensor created, `mask_cond` is shape [tgt_len]. And `.view(tgt_len, 1)` makes it [tgt_len, 1]. Let me call it `col = torch.arange(tgt_len).view(tgt_len, 1)`. Then `mask_cond` is `row = torch.arange(tgt_len)` shape [tgt_len]. `mask.masked_fill_(row < col + 1, 0)`: positions where `row < col + 1` are set to 0. For entry (i, j): - row = i, col = j - `i < j + 1` → `i <= j` - This sets mask(i, j) = 0 when i <= j - And mask stays at `torch.finfo.min` when i > j Wait, that means the mask is 0 in the upper triangle (including diagonal) and very negative in the lower triangle? That's the opposite of what we want! Or is it? Let me check: if `mask(i, j) = 0` for `i <= j`, and `min` for `i > j`. Actually in PyTorch broadcasting: - `mask_cond` shape: [tgt_len] - `(mask_cond + 1).view(tgt_len, 1)` shape: [tgt_len, 1] - Comparison: left is broadcasted to [tgt_len, tgt_len], right is broadcasted to [tgt_len, tgt_len] - left[i, j] = mask_cond[j] = j - right[i, j] = (mask_cond + 1)[i] = i + 1 - So result[i, j] = (j < i + 1) = (j <= i) Yes, exactly. However, there might be another issue: shared memory bank conflicts. `k_tile[row * D + col]` where D=64. With 32 threads in a warp, if they all read different `row` but same `col`, the addresses are `row * 64 + col`, spaced by 64. Shared memory has 32 banks, and each bank is 4 bytes. Address `addr` maps to bank `addr % 32`. So `row * 64 + col` maps to bank `(row * 64 + col) % 32 = col % 32` (since 64 is divisible by 32). If all threads in a warp read the same `col` but different `row`, they all access the same bank! That's a 32-way bank conflict. Very bad. Wait, in our dot product loop: ```cpp for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { dot += q_vals[d] * k_tile[(key_pos - tile_start) * D + d]; } ``` All threads in the warp read `k_tile[(key_pos - tile_start) * D + d]` for the same `key_pos` and same `d` but different `d`? No, wait. `d` is the loop counter. In iteration `d`, all threads read `k_tile[... * D + d]` for the same value of `d` (since `d` is the same for all threads in the warp). So all 32 threads in a warp are reading the exact same address `k_tile[(key_pos - tile_start) * D + d]`! This is a broadcast, which does NOT cause bank conflicts! CUDA handles broadcasts efficiently by reading from shared memory once and broadcasting to all threads. So no bank conflict here! Wait, that's amazing. Because all threads in the warp are computing the same key position at the same time (since `key_pos` is the same for all threads), they all read the same K element. That's a perfect broadcast. And `q_vals[d]` is per-thread (different `q_vals` per thread), which is in registers, so no shared memory access. Then for `v_tile`: ```cpp o[d] = (o[d] * l * exp_m_diff + exp_dot * v_tile[(key_pos - tile_start) * D + d]) / l_new; ``` Again, all threads read `v_tile[... * D + d]` for the same `d` and same `key_pos`. Broadcast! Perfect. So there are NO shared memory bank conflicts in the inner loops. This is an excellent design. For loading K and V tiles into shared memory: ```cpp for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < tile_size * D; idx += blockDim.x) { int row = idx / D; int col = idx % D; k_tile[row * D + col] = ...; } ``` Thread 0 writes `k_tile[0]`, thread 1 writes `k_tile[1]`, thread 2 writes `k_tile[2]`, etc. These are consecutive addresses, so they map to banks 0, 1, 2, ... which are all different. No bank conflicts. Perfect. And for the output write: ```cpp out_ptr[d] = o[d]; ``` Each thread writes to a different row and the same columns, but consecutive columns are written by consecutive threads in consecutive iterations? Actually, the write is in a loop: ```cpp for (int d = 0; d < D; d++) { out_ptr[d] = o[d]; } ``` In iteration `d`, thread `s` writes to `out_ptr[d]`. Since `out_ptr = out + b * stride_b + s * stride_s + h * D`, thread `s` writes to address `out_base + s * stride_s + d`. With `stride_s = H * D = 16 * 64 = 1024`, threads write to addresses spaced by 1024 bytes. Memory coalescing requires threads in a warp to write to consecutive addresses. Here, thread `s` writes to `out_base + s * 1024 + d`. For a warp of 32 threads, these addresses are spaced by 1024 bytes. Each warp accesses 32 * 1024 bytes = 32KB. But consecutive threads access addresses 1KB apart. This is NOT coalesced! Actually, wait. In the write loop, `d` is the loop variable. For a fixed `d`, all threads in the warp write to different rows. The stride between thread `s` and `s+1` is `stride_s = H * D = 1024` floats = 4096 bytes. But memory transactions happen in 32-byte or 128-byte segments. So each thread in the warp is writing to a different segment. This requires 32 separate memory transactions. Very inefficient. To fix this, I should have threads within a warp write to consecutive memory. But since each thread handles a different query position, and the data layout is [B, S, H, D] with S as the second fastest-changing dimension after D, consecutive S positions for the same H and D are adjacent in memory? Wait: For layout [B, S, H, D], offset = b * S * H * D + s * H * D + h * D + d Consecutive s have offsets differing by H * D = 1024 floats = 4096 bytes. So they are far apart. If I want coalesced writes, I should consider a different data layout or a different thread assignment. Actually, in the attention kernel, if I process the output differently... Let me think. The standard [B, S, H, D] layout means if I process by (B, H) blocks and S threads, then for output writes, consecutive threads write to different S positions. This is strided and not coalesced. To get coalesced writes, I could: 1. Transpose the output so that [B, H, S, D] where consecutive s are adjacent. Actually, the projection is: [B*S, hidden] @ [hidden, hidden] = [B*S, hidden]. This gives us [B*S, H*D]. To get [B, H, S, D], we need to: 1. View to [B, S, H, D] 2. Permute to [B, H, S, D] 3. Make contiguous This involves a copy. In the reference code, `BartAttention` does exactly this. So there's no way to avoid the transpose unless we fuse the projection into a different format. But for our custom attention kernel, what layout do we actually need? If Q, K, V are in [B, H, S, D] layout and contiguous: - offset = b * H * S * D + h * S * D + s * D + d - Consecutive s are adjacent (stride D = 64 floats = 256 bytes) - For our kernel where threads handle different s but same (b, h, d), consecutive threads writing to the same d but different s would be coalesced (stride 256 bytes within a 128-byte cache line... wait, 256 bytes is 2 cache lines. So thread s and s+1 write to addresses 256 bytes apart. For a warp of 32 threads, they write to addresses spanning 32 * 256 = 8192 bytes = 128 * 64 bytes. That's 128-byte lines, so 64 memory transactions? No. Actually, for coalescing, consecutive threads should access consecutive addresses. If thread s accesses offset `base + s * D * sizeof(float)`, that's strided by D = 64 floats = 256 bytes. 256 bytes is exactly 2 L2 cache lines (128 bytes each). For a warp writing 32 floats (128 bytes) per thread, total per warp is 4096 bytes. If threads write at stride 256 bytes, then warp writes to 32 addresses: base, base+256, base+512, ..., base+7936. In terms of 128-byte lines, these are every other line. So 16 lines are accessed. Since a memory transaction can handle multiple lines, this might still be reasonably efficient. But it's not perfectly coalesced. Actually, for modern GPUs (Ampere+), the coalescing unit can handle some strided patterns efficiently. And with 256-byte stride, consecutive threads hit different memory banks (since 256 / 32 = 8 bytes per bank for float32, which is 2 floats). Actually, L1 cache lines are 128 bytes and sectors are 32 bytes. With 32 threads in a warp accessing 32 addresses spaced by 256 bytes = 64 floats: - Address 0: sector 0 - Address 256: sector 8 (since 256/32 = 8) - Address 512: sector 16 - Address 768: sector 24 - Address 1024: sector 0 of next line... So the warp accesses sectors 0, 8, 16, 24,... across many cache lines. This is actually a broadcast-like pattern to different sectors and lines, requiring many transactions. To get perfect coalescing, we want consecutive threads to access consecutive floats (address = base + tid * 4 bytes). That means we want thread `s` to handle something where the output address increments by 4 bytes per thread. One way to achieve this: instead of having each thread handle one query position, have each thread handle one element of the output (one d for one s). But then the softmax needs to be computed across keys for a single query, which requires synchronization. That's much more complex. For our first attempt, the imperfect coalescing is probably acceptable, especially since the output write is only 64 floats per thread (256 bytes), and the dominant cost is likely the attention computation itself. Actually, let me reconsider whether I should use [B, S, H, D] or [B, H, S, D] in my kernel. In my current plan, I use [B, S, H, D] (no permute). So `self.config.vocab_size` should work. But wait, there's a subtle bug. In the `fused_residual_layernorm_forward` call, I pass `self.buf_out` and `hidden`. But `self.buf_out` is allocated as [B, S, hidden_size]. And `hidden` is also [B, S, hidden_size]. The kernel operates on `N = B * S` rows. But what if `B` and `S` in the forward call differ from the pre-allocated buffer sizes? The reference says batch_size = 32, sequence_length = 256. But the `forward` method should handle any shape. If I pre-allocate for 32x256 and receive different sizes, it crashes. For safety, I could dynamically allocate or check sizes. But the problem says `get_inputs()` returns exactly `(32, 256)`. And typical benchmarks use fixed sizes. Still, to be robust: Actually, looking at the problem description, it says "Implement forward() with the same signature." This suggests the signature should handle variable inputs. But `get_inputs()` is fixed. For safety, I'll handle any size but fall back to PyTorch if the size is larger than pre-allocated buffers, or just allocate dynamically. Actually, let me just not pre-allocate. PyTorch's caching allocator handles repeated allocations quite well for fixed sizes. The overhead of allocating 5-6 tensors per layer is maybe 5 microseconds * 60 allocations = 300 microseconds. Not huge. If I pre-allocate for standard sizes and reallocate if needed, that's a good compromise. Let me keep the pre-allocation but add a check: ```python if self.buf_q.shape[0] != B or self.buf_q.shape[1] != S: self.buf_q = torch.empty(B, S, self.num_heads, self.head_dim, device='cuda', dtype=torch.float32) # ... reallocate all buffers ``` This adds a small overhead but handles any input size. Also, I notice I used `self.config.vocab_size` in the LM head, but `self.config` is the config object. That's fine. One issue: `custom_cuda.layernorm_forward` returns a new tensor. For the embedding layer norm, I do `hidden = custom_cuda.layernorm_forward(...)`. This replaces `hidden`. Good. But for the `layernorm_kernel`, I used `extern __shared__ float smem[];` and then `float* s_sum = smem; float* s_sq_sum = smem + num_threads;`. But `num_threads` is a runtime variable. In CUDA, `extern __shared__` doesn't have a fixed size, so adding `num_threads` (which is `blockDim.x`) is fine. I'll allocate `2 * threads * sizeof(float)` in the host launcher. That matches. Wait, in the `layernorm_kernel`, I used `float* s_sq_sum = smem + num_threads;`. If blockDim.x = 256, then s_sq_sum = smem + 256. And I allocate `2 * 256 * sizeof(float) = 2048` bytes. That's correct. For `fused_residual_layernorm_kernel`, same thing. For `causal_attention_kernel`, I used `extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* k_tile = smem; float* v_tile = smem + TILE_SIZE * D;`. And I allocate `2 * TILE_SIZE * D * sizeof(float)`. If D=64, that's `2 * 64 * 64 * 4 = 32768`. Correct. One thing I should be careful about: the `fused_residual_layernorm_kernel` accesses `weight[i]` where `i` is the column index and `tid` is the thread index. But `weight` is a 1D tensor of size C. Different threads access `weight[i]` for potentially different `i`. Since threads in a warp might access `weight[0], weight[1], weight[2], ...` if `tid` aligns, but actually `i = tid + k * num_threads`, so consecutive threads access consecutive `weight` elements. This is coalesced. Good. Similarly for `bias[i]`. For the attention kernel output write: thread `s` writes to `out_ptr[d]` for `d = 0..D-1`. These are consecutive addresses in memory (since D is the fastest dimension). But consecutive threads access addresses `base_s + d` where `base_s` differs by `stride_s = H*D`. So it's strided by `H*D` elements = 1024 floats = 4096 bytes. On modern GPUs, global memory accesses with strides up to a certain size can still be coalesced into a single transaction if they fall within the same cache line. With 4096-byte stride, each thread hits a different cache line (lines are 128 bytes). So 32 separate cache lines are needed, which may require 32 memory transactions. This is bad. To optimize this, I could write to shared memory first in a coalesced pattern and then have all threads write to global memory in a coalesced way. But that requires a transpose. Specifically: 1. Each thread writes its output `o[0..D-1]` to shared memory in its own slot 2. After all threads finish, the block writes from shared memory to global memory in a coalesced manner But `__syncthreads()` is needed between computation and writing. The shared memory layout could be: for each d, store all S outputs. Thread s writes its o[d] to `out_smem[d * S + s]`. Then after sync, threads write global memory with consecutive threads accessing consecutive addresses: For address `global_out[b, s, h, d]`, if we reorder to `global_out[b, h, d, s]`, consecutive s would be adjacent. But we want [B, S, H, D] output. Or, threads can write in groups. For chunk of S positions being written to the same (b, h, d), consecutive threads can handle different s values but adjacent in memory... wait, in [B, S, H, D], for fixed (b, h, d), the addresses for s=0,1,2,... are spaced by H*D*4 bytes. So they're not adjacent. Actually, NO two different s values for the same (b, h, d) are adjacent in memory. The adjacent memory addresses correspond to different d values. So for coalesced writes, consecutive threads should write consecutive d values for the same (b, s, h). In our current kernel, each thread writes all D values for its own s. If consecutive threads write d=0 for s=0, s=1, s=2, etc., these are addresses spaced by H*D = 4096 bytes. Bad. If instead, we have a transpose step where we gather: - Warp 0 writes d=0 for s=0..31 - But s=0..31 for d=0 are at addresses: base + s * H*D + 0. These are still spaced by 4096 bytes. Hmm, in [B, S, H, D] layout, it's fundamentally impossible to have consecutive s values adjacent in memory. Adjacent memory corresponds to consecutive d values. To get coalescing for writes across s, I need to change the output layout to [B, H, S, D] or [B, H, D, S]. If I output [B, H, S, D], then for fixed (b, h), consecutive s are adjacent (stride D = 64 floats = 256 bytes). This is better but still strided. If I output [B, H, D, S], then consecutive s are perfectly adjacent (stride 1 float = 4 bytes). This is perfectly coalesced! But then I need the rest of the pipeline to handle this layout. The output projection does [B*S, hidden] @ [hidden, hidden]. If the attention output is stored as [B, H, D, S], viewing it to [B*S, H*D] might not be straightforward because H*D = hidden, and we need the elements corresponding to each (b, s) to be contiguous. In [B, H, D, S], the elements for a fixed (b, s) are at positions (b, :, :, s), which are not contiguous. So viewing to [B*S, hidden] would require a copy. So [B, H, S, D] is the natural layout for viewing to [B*S, H*D] where elements for each (b, s) are contiguous. In [B, H, S, D], for fixed (b, s), the elements (b, 0, s, 0..D-1), (b, 1, s, 0..D-1), ..., (b, H-1, s, 0..D-1) are contiguous because the H dimension is between S and D. Wait: [B, H, S, D]: offset = b*H*S*D + h*S*D + s*D + d For fixed b, s: varying h and d gives offset = b*H*S*D + s*D + h*S*D + d This is NOT contiguous because the h*S*D term has a large stride (S*D = 256*64 = 16384 floats = 64KB). Elements for different h are far apart. So [B, H, S, D] cannot be viewed to [B*S, H*D] without a transpose. In fact, to get [B*S, H*D] contiguous, we need [B, S, H, D]! In [B, S, H, D]: offset = b*S*H*D + s*H*D + h*D + d For fixed b, s: varying h and d gives offset = b*S*H*D + s*H*D + (h*D + d). As h goes from 0 to H-1 and d from 0 to D-1, h*D + d goes from 0 to H*D-1. So it's contiguous! Great, so [B, S, H, D] is the correct layout for viewing to [B*S, hidden]. And that's what I already have. The downside is that for the attention write, consecutive threads (varying s) write to addresses spaced by H*D*4 = 4096 bytes. This is bad for coalescing. But since each thread writes 64 floats, the total write per thread is 256 bytes, and the block writes 64KB. With 512 blocks, total write is 32MB for the output. At 3.35TB/s, even with poor coalescing, it might take ~0.1ms or less. Actually, if each warp needs 32 memory transactions for its writes (instead of 1-2 with perfect coalescing), the write time could be 10-20x longer. 32MB with 20x overhead = 640MB of effective traffic. At 3.35TB/s = 0.19ms. Still not huge compared to the whole model. For simplicity, I'll keep the current layout. I can optimize later if needed. Let me check another thing: in `fused_residual_layernorm_forward`, I pass `hidden` which gets overwritten each layer. But the `residual` input is also `hidden` from the previous step. Wait, in my code: ```python hidden = custom_cuda.fused_residual_layernorm_forward(self.buf_out, hidden, ...) ``` Here, `self.buf_out` is x (the new activation), and `hidden` is the residual. The kernel computes `layer_norm(buf_out + hidden)`. Then assigns the result to `hidden`. So `hidden` is updated.
